## Question 1 (Year: 2018 | Paper: GS III)
**Question ID: 2018-gs3-q1-civilsdaily**

** How are the principles followed by NITI Aayog different from those followed by the erstwhile planning commission in India? [Year: 2018] [Marks: 15]**

### Answer

NITI Aayog, established in **2015 **, replaced the Planning Commission to reflect India’s transition from a ** centralized planned economy **to a ** market-led, cooperative federalist model **.
** Key Differences Between Planning Commission and NITI Aayog**

|** Planning Commission **|** NITI Aayog **|
| Followed ** centralized planning **based on command-control economic principles. | Follows ** market-driven **, ** evidence-based **, ** reform-oriented **principles. |
| Practiced ** top-down federalism **| Promotes ** cooperative and competitive federalism **|
|** Allocation of funds **to states | Acts as a ** think tank **, does not allocate funds. |
| Emphasis on ** Five-Year Plans **, rigid targets. | Emphasis on ** long-term vision **, ** strategic reforms **, ** outcome monitoring **(e.g., SDG Index). |
|** Monolithic **, bureaucratic, centralized by design. |** Flexible **, with involvement of experts, domain specialists, and states. |
|** one-size-fits-all **approach. | Tailored strategies. Eg-** Aspirational Districts**
** Programme **|
| Limited focus on real-time evaluation | Strong focus on ** data-driven governance **, real-time dashboards. |
| Minimal involvement of private sector, academia, civil society. | Encourages ** multi-stakeholder consultations **for policy formulation. |

### Similarities Between NITI Aayog and Planning Commission

1.    National Development Objective
2.    Advisory Role to Government
3.    Coordination Function
4.    Focus on Long-term Vision
5.    Multisectoral Engagement

### Importance of of NITI Aayog

1.    Reflects shift from state-led to market-led development model
2.    Improves Centre-State cooperation for faster execution
3.    Enhances accountability and outcome-based governance
4.    Encourages policy experimentation and innovation
NITI Aayog reflects India’s evolving needs as a **21st-century, globally integrated economy **.

---

## Question 2 (Year: 2020 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2020-gs3-q2-civilsdaily**

** Define potential GDP and explain its determinants. What are the factors that have been inhibiting India from realizing its potential GDP? [Year: 2020] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

** Potential GDP **refers to the ** maximum sustainable output **an economy can produce ** without generating inflationary pressure **, when all resources are fully and efficiently employed.

### Determinants of Potential GDP

1.    Labour Force & Human Capital - Size, skill, and productivity of the workforce.
2.    Capital Formation - Investment in infrastructure, machinery, and technology.
3.    Technology & Innovation - R&D and digital transformation driving productivity.
4.    Institutional Quality - Governance, regulatory efficiency, and property rights.
5.    Total Factor Productivity (TFP) - Efficiency in using labour and capital together.
6.    Prevailing Inflation Rate - Persistent inflation distorts real GDP from its potential level.
7.    Global Conditions - Protectionism, trade restrictions, and geopolitical tensions. Eg- Tariff Wars

### Factors Inhibiting India from Realizing Potential GDP

1.    Low Female Labour Force Participation - FLFPR only 41.7% (PLFS) against global average of 48%
2.    Slow Capital Formation - GFCF at ~29.6% of GDP (2024) vs 34% in 2023.
3.    Skill Mismatch & Education Gaps - Only 4.7% of workforce formally skilled (NSDC).
4.    Infrastructure Bottlenecks - Logistics cost ~13% of GDP vs 8% in USA
5.    Weak Productivity Growth - Low TFP and informal sector dominance. (83% informal sector)
6.    Regulatory Cholesterol - Delays, compliance burden, weak contract enforcement.

### Way Forward

1.    Enhance Human Capital - Invest in education, healthcare, and skill development
2.    Accelerate Investment & Infrastructure Growth through faster project execution under PPP.
3.    Create safe workplaces, flexible jobs, and childcare support to tap women’s economic potential.
4.    Increase R&D spending to 2.5% of GDP (currently <1% of GDP) for productivity gains.
To realize its ** potential GDP and Viksit Bharat 2047 **, India must shift from ** factor accumulation **to ** productivity-driven growth**

---

## Question 3 (Year: 2021 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2021-gs3-q3-civilsdaily**

** Explain the difference between computing methodology of India’s Gross Domestic Product (GDP) before the year 2015 and after the year 2015. [Year: 2021] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

** Gross Domestic Product (GDP) **is the ** total monetary value of all final goods and services **produced within a country’s borders in a given period. GDP in India is calculated by the ** National Statistical Office (NSO) **.

|** Parameter **|** Before 2015 Methodology **|** After 2015 Methodology **|
|** Base Year **|**2004-05 **|**2011-12 **(reflecting modern economy) |
|** GDP Measurement **|** GDP at Factor Cost **|** GDP at Market Prices **(includes taxes, subtracts subsidies) |
|** International Standard Followed **|** UN-SNA 1993 **|** UN-SNA 2008 **|
|** Corporate Sector Data **| ASI + sample surveys |** MCA-21 database **covering 5+ lakh companies |
|** Manufacturing Sector Measurement **| Production-based estimates |** Value Added + Double-Deflation Method **|

|** Price Indices for Real GDP **| Mostly ** WPI **| Combination of ** CPI + WPI + sectoral indices **|
|** Financial Sector Coverage **| Mostly banks |** NBFCs, Mutual Funds, Insurance, Market data **|
|** Services Sector Coverage **| Limited |** Expanded IT, telecom, real estate, digital services **|
|** Informal Sector Estimation **| Older NSSO data | Updated ** NSSO surveys + improved estimation techniques **|

The post-2015 GDP methodology aims to provide a ** more accurate, data-rich, and globally comparable **picture of India’s economy. To improve reliability, there is a need for greater transparency.

---

## Question 4 (Year: 2023 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2023-gs3-q4-civilsdaily**

** Most of the unemployment in India is structural in nature. Examine the methodology adopted to compute unemployment in the country and suggest improvements. [Year: 2023] [Marks: 15]**

### Answer

** Structural unemployment **occurs when workers ** lack the skills, education, or geographic mobility **required to match available jobs. In India, it reflects a ** mismatch **between the workforce’s capabilities and the evolving needs of a modern economy.

### Why Unemployment is Structural in India

1.    Skill Mismatch - Majority of workforce is low-skilled; only ~4.7% formally skilled (NSDC).
2.    Agriculture Dependence - 49% workforce in agriculture producing 16-17% of GDP
3.    Slow Growth of Labour-Intensive Industries - Manufacturing unable to absorb labour at scale.
4.    Automation and Digitalisation - Eg- AI, Robotics leading to job losses
5.    Low Female Labour Participation - FLFPR at 41.7% (PLFS 2023-24) due to social norms, skill gaps, and lack of suitable jobs.
6.    Regional Imbalances - Job clusters in southern/western India vs labour concentration in BIMARU states.
7.    Informalization of economy - 89% of workforce in informal sector.

### Methodology to Compute Unemployment in India

1.    NSSO (under MOSPI) is the principal body responsible for estimating unemployment.
2.    Periodic Labour Force Survey (PLFS) - NSO measures unemployment through three indicators:
•    Usual Status (US/PS+SS) - Based on activity over 365 days
•    Current Weekly Status (CWS) - If not worked for 1 hour in the last 7 days.
•    Current Daily Status (CDS) - Records activity for each day of last week - best for informal/underemployment.
3.    Household Surveys - Annual (rural + urban) and quarterly (urban) surveys.
4.    Establishment Surveys
•    QES for formal sector
•    ASI for organised manufacturing
5.    Administrative Data - EPFO, ESIC, NPS payrolls used to estimate formal job creation.
6.    Unemployment rate = No. of unemployed persons / Total labour force

### Issues with Current Methodology

1.    Underestimation of Informal Sector - ~90% workforce informal. PLFS & enterprise surveys do not capture home-based, gig, or platform work fully.
2.    Surveys don’t map job requirements vs worker skills, essential for assessing structural unemployment.
3.    Low Frequency - Eg- PLFS rural data is measured annually
4.    Urban Bias - Quarterly surveys are confined to urban areas. Rural distress is under-measured.
5.    Limited Coverage - Gig economy, digital services, start-ups, and EV/green jobs not adequately represented.

### Way Forward

1.    Use Big Data Analytics to gather real-time analysis.
2.    Incorporate ‘underemployment’ into the definition of unemployment.
3.    Timely release of data.
4.    Increase Frequency - Monthly or quarterly surveys for rural areas
5.    Align with International Standards (ILO + SNA 2025)- Update definitions to include multi-job holders, remote workers, freelancers, and platform-based workers.
Improving methodology is essential to generate accurate employment estimates and design stronger job creation policies.

---

## Question 5 (Year: 2023 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2023-gs3-q5-civilsdaily**

** Distinguish between ‘care economy’ and ‘monetized economy’. How can care economy be brought into monetized economy through women empowerment? [Year: 2023] [Marks: 15]**

### Answer

** Care economy **refers to the ** unpaid or underpaid activities **performed within households and communities such as childcare, elderly care, household maintenance, and emotional labour. In contrast, the ** monetized economy **includes ** all paid economic activities **that generate income

### Key Features of the Care Economy:

•    Human-Centered
•    Labour-Intensive
•    Majorly Informal Sector

### Fact File

1.    The contribution of care work to India’s GDP is around 15-17%
2.    Women contribute 84% of unpaid care work.
3.    Global care investments can create 475 million jobs by 2030.

|** Care Economy **|** Monetized Economy **|
| Unpaid caregiving, domestic work, emotional labour | Paid labour producing goods and services |
| Not counted in GDP or national accounts | Direct contributor to GDP and economic growth |
| Largely invisible, undervalued | Highly visible and economically recognised |
| Performed mostly by ** women **| Higher participation of ** men **|
| Informal, unrecognized skills | Formal skills, training and certifications |
| No wages or financial return | Generates income and employment |
| Seen as natural duty or responsibility | Seen as productive economic activity |

### Bringing Care Economy into Monetized Economy through Women Empowerment

1.    Raising investment in the care economy to 2% (>1 % current) can generate 11 million jobs for women.

2.    Draw lessons from Japan’s womenomics for boosting female labour participation.
3.    Encourage PPP models and CSR initiatives to expand affordable care infrastructure.
4.    Skill Development and Certification in childcare, geriatric care, nursing, early education, nutrition.
5.    Formalisation of Care Services by expanding creches, Anganwadis, elderly care centres, community caregiving services.
6.    Promotion of Women-led Care Entrepreneurship such as day-care centres, tiffin services by providing credit, digital platforms, SHG support, and market linkages.
7.    Social Protection- Recognising care work under minimum wage laws and social security frameworks.
8.    Digital platforms like online caregiving services, home-nursing apps, domestic work registries help women monetise care skills.
9.    Investments in time-saving infrastructure such as clean cooking fuel, piped water, and public transport can increase FLFPR in the formal economy.
** Adopting the 3R Framework (Recognize, Reduce and Redistribute) can help realise SDG 5.4.**

---

## Question 6 (Year: 2018 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2018-gs3-q6-civilsdaily**

** How would the recent phenomena of protectionism and currency manipulations in world trade affect macroeconomic stability of India? [Year: 2018] [Marks: 15]**

### Answer

The rising ** protectionism **and ** currency manipulations **have disrupted global trade flows and have direct implications for ** India’s **growth, inflation, fiscal balance, and external vulnerability.

### Tools of Protectionism

1.    Tariffs
2.    Quotas
3.    Import Licensing
4.    Sanctions
5.    Exchange Controls
6.    Industrial Subsidies

### Impact of Protectionism on Macroeconomic Stability of India

1.    Export Slowdown due to high tariffs. Eg: US protectionism under Section 232 hurt India’s steel exports.
2.    Supply Chain Disruptions lead to higher Production Costs. Eg- higher oil prices after Israel-Palestine conflict
3.    Imported Inflation due to barriers on food, energy and intermediate goods. Eg: Indonesia palm oil ban.
4.    Weak Employment in Export-oriented Sectors - Eg: Fall in European demand hit India’s textile and leather clusters.
5.    Lower FDI Inflows - Uncertain trade regimes discourage long-term investments. Eg- Apple cancelling plant in India after Trump threat.

### Impact of Currency Manipulations on Macroeconomic Stability

1.    Widening Trade Deficit - Undervalued currencies make their exports cheaper. Eg- China’s managed yuan
2.    Rupee Volatility creates monetary Policy Challenges. Eg: Yen depreciation in 2023-24 triggered pressure on Asian currencies including INR.
3.    Higher Inflation and BoP Pressure - Eg: INR touching 83-84 per USD raised petroleum import bills.
4.    Capital Outflows due to dollar strengthening. Eg: 2022-24 saw FPI outflows during phases of aggressive US Fed tightening.
5.    Pressure on Forex Reserves - Eg: RBI sold USD in 2022-23 to stabilise INR, reducing reserves temporarily.

### Opportunities for India Amid Protectionism & Currency Politics

1.    China+1 Advantage in electronics, chemicals, renewables. Eg- Mobile exports crossed USD 11 bn in 2023-24.
2.    Boost Make in India to build self-reliant supply chains. Eg: PLI schemes in semiconductors, textiles, solar modules.
3.    Diversification of Trade Partners - Eg- Recent FTA with UK
4.    Strategic Attractiveness as a Stable Market - Amid volatile currencies and geo-economic blocs, India is seen as a stable investment destination.
5.    Promoting Rupee Trade Mechanisms - Eg- INR invoicing and Vostro accounts.
6.    Opportunity to Lead on Fair Trade Norms in WTO, G20 on currency transparency and non-tariff barriers.

### Way Forward

1.    Enhance R&D (2.5% of GDP), reduce logistics costs (PM Gati Shakti), and expand PLI schemes to boost manufacturing resilience.
2.    Accelerate FTAs with EU, GCC to reduce over-dependence on a few partners.
3.    Strengthen FOREX buffers and expand rupee trade settlement
4.    Encourage domestic production of critical inputs (electronics, APIs, green tech) to reduce vulnerability to global shocks.
5.    Scale IT, fintech, health tourism, education services to offset goods-trade shocks from rising protectionism.
By strengthening domestic competitiveness, India can position itself as a ** reliable, rules-based and resilient player in the evolving global economic order **.

---

## Question 7 (Year: 2019 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2019-gs3-q7-civilsdaily**

** Do you agree with the view that steady GDP growth and low inflation have left the Indian economy in good shape? Give reasons in support of your arguments. [Year: 2019] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

India is projected to sustain GDP growth of **6.5%** between ** FY28-30, ** positioning it as the world's ** third-largest consumer market** by 2026 and the ** third-largest economy** by 2028. (UBS)

### Arguments Supporting the View (Indian economy in good shape)

1.    High GDP Growth - India remains the fastest-growing major economy. 7% in FY 2025.
2.    Moderating Inflation - Eg- Retail inflation fell to a historic low of 0.25% in October 2025, due to GST rate cuts

3.    Forex reserves at over $689 billion provide external stability.
4.    Fiscal Consolidation Path- Fiscal deficit targeted to reduce to 4.8% of GDP in 2025-26.
5.    Robust Financial Sector- Gross NPAs have declined from 9.11% (2021) to 2.8% (2025).
6.    Production-linked incentives (PLI) has raised India’s manufacturing attractiveness. Eg: Electronics exports at a record $38 billion in 2024-25. (32% increase)

### Arguments Against the View (Macro vulnerabilities persist)

1.    Jobless Growth -Service sector contributes 55% of GDP but employs less than 30% workforce
2.    High food inflation due to climate shocks, hurting the poor.
3.    Rural Distress due to weak agriculture real wages and uneven monsoons.
4.    Global slowdown, protectionism, and China’s dominance limit India’s merchandise exports.
5.    High Public Debt- General government debt remains around 82% of GDP (IMF, 2024), limiting fiscal room.
6.    The share of Gross Fixed Capital Formation (GFCF) was about 34.6% of GDP in 2023-24 and slipped to 29.61% of GDP in 2024, indicating weak investment.

### Way Forward

1.    Enhance R&D (2.5% of GDP), reduce logistics costs (PM Gati Shakti), and expand PLI schemes to boost manufacturing.

2.    Promote labour-intensive manufacturing (textiles, toys, food processing) and expand services exports (IT, GBS, health tourism).
3.    Improve ease of doing business, accelerate contract enforcement, and reduce regulatory uncertainty to crowd-in private capital.
4.    Strengthen FOREX buffers and expand rupee trade settlement
5.    Encourage domestic production of critical inputs (electronics, APIs, green tech) to reduce vulnerability to global shocks.
As highlighted by the Economic Survey, India must prioritise ** blue-sky thinking** and foster a ** virtuous cycle of investment** to achieve Viksit Bharat@2047.

---

## Question 8 (Year: 2022 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2022-gs3-q8-civilsdaily**

** Economic growth in the recent past has been led by increase in labour activity.” Explain this statement. Suggest the growth pattern that will lead to creation of more jobs without compromising labour productivity. [Year: 2022] [Marks: 15]**

### Answer

With **7% growth in 2025-26 **, India is one of the ** fastest-growing major economies **and a ** bright spot **on the global economy **(IMF) **. A major driver of this performance has been the ** expansion in labour activity.**

### Economic growth attributed to labour activity

1.    Demographic dividend - Median age of 28 and 65% working-age population (65%) has increased labour supply and productive capacity.
2.    Shift towards labour-intensive sectors: Growth in construction, retail, transportation, tourism, gig and platform economy
3.    Surge in self-employment - from 52% (2017) to 58% in 2024 (PLFS data)
4.    The government's skilling push through Kaushal Vikas Yojana and the Skill India Mission improved workforce capabilities.
5.    India becoming the 3rd largest start-up ecosystem has generated new entrepreneurship-led employment.
6.    Rise of gig economy- Platform-based work has widened job opportunities.
7.    Labour Code reforms- consolidation of labour laws has improved hiring flexibility and EoDB.

### Other reasons

•    GST reforms
•    Ease of Doing Business reforms
•    IBC
•    PLI schemes

### However, this growth pattern is problematic due to

1.    Low productivity trap: Most new jobs are in informal, low-wage, low-productivity sectors.
2.    Disguised employment rising: Higher labour supply masks underemployment.
3.    Limited wage growth: High labour participation has not translated into better wages.
4.    Structural transformation incomplete: Manufacturing’s share in jobs and GDP remains stagnant.

### Suggested Growth Pattern to Create More Jobs Without Compromising Productivity

1.    Manufacturing-led, technology-enabled growth
•    Expand labour-intensive manufacturing such as textiles, toys, leather, electronics assembly. Eg: PLI schemes for electronics, textiles.
•    Use AI, robotics, lean production to improve productivity while expanding scale.
2.    MSME upgradation - Enable cluster-based development, digitalisation, easier credit. Eg: MSME Champions Scheme, ONDC for market linkages
3.    Skill-based job creation through programs like Skill India, PMKVY 4.0.
4.    Boost food processing, millets, horticulture, and FPO-based value chains.
5.    Employment in solar manufacturing, EV ecosystem, recycling, energy efficiency can raise both jobs and productivity.
6.    Strengthen urban employment ecosystems - Invest in urban infrastructure, housing, logistics, and city industrial clusters.
7.    Improve FLFPR through childcare support, flexible work, safety, and skilling.
India’s recent growth has been driven more by ** labour mobilisation **than by ** labour productivity **. A shift towards ** manufacturing-led, technology-driven, and green growth **is essential for ** Viksit Bharat 2047.**

---

## Question 9 (Year: 2025 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2025-gs3-q9-civilsdaily**

** What are the challenges before the Indian economy when the world is moving away from free trade and multilateralism to protectionism and bilateralism? How can these challenges be met? [Year: 2025] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

** According to the Economic Survey, the previous global paradigm of ‘stable geopolitics’ and ‘free trade and investment movement’, has been fading and the foundations on which many nations built themselves are now**
** being shaken.**

### World Moving from Free Trade & Multilateralism to Protectionism & Bilateralism

1.    Trade Wars - US-China tariff wars
2.    WTO Deadlock over Doha Development Agenda
3.    Rise of Bilateral/Regional Deals - Eg- RCEP
4.    Green Protectionism - EU’s Carbon Border Adjustment Mechanism (CBAM), US CHIPS Act

### Challenges before the Indian economy

1.    Deglobalisation Trends - Shift towards self-reliance in advanced economies hampers multilateralism. Eg- Eg-US Inflation Reduction Act

Global Trade Policy Uncertainty Index

Source: Global Trade Policy Uncertainty Index
www.econovis.net (X)@>econovisuals ^
2.    Fragmentation of Global Trade due to rise in tariffs, sanctions etc threaten export-oriented sectors. Eg- IT Industry
3.    Volatile Capital Flows - US Fed rate hikes have led to ₹1.2 lakh crore outflows from Indian markets (FY 2024).
4.    Energy security challenges due to sanctions on Russia (40% share)
5.    Currency Depreciation - INR fell to an all-time low of ₹88/$ (2025), increasing import-led inflation.
6.    Technology Barriers - New protectionist tools like data localisation rules of EU.
7.    Employment Impact - Labour-intensive sectors like textiles, gems, and automobiles face slowdown.

### Way Forward

### Internal Measures

1.    Ease of Doing Business - The Economic Survey (2024-25) key recommendation is ‘to get the domestic economic engine purring by pulling all the levers of deregulation’.
2.    Raising the investment rate to around 35% of GDP from the current level of ~ 31%.
3.    Boost domestic demand through high public capex
4.    Build resilience in semiconductors, defence, and critical minerals under Atmanirbhar Bharat.

### External Measures (Global Integration)

1.    Diversify Export Markets - Expand trade with Africa, Latin America, Central Asia, and ASEAN.
2.    Conclude Balanced FTAs - With EU, Canada, Australia.

3.    Strengthen IMEC, INSTC, and Chabahar Port for secure and cost-effective routes.
4.    Global South Leadership in WTO to revive dispute settlement and ensure fair rules.
A ** self-sustained growth strategy **is imperative for India’s long-term economic sovereignty.

## Question 10 (Year: 2019 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2019-gs3-q10-civilsdaily**

** It is argued that the strategy of inclusive growth is intended to meet the objectives of inclusiveness and sustainability together. Comment on this statement. [Year: 2019] [Marks: 15]**

### Answer

As per ** OECD **, inclusive growth is economic growth distributed fairly across society and creates opportunities for all.

### Fact File

1.    Extreme poverty fell from 16.2 % in 2011-12 to just 2.3 % in 2022-23

### Inclusive Growth Promoting Inclusiveness

1.    Expands economic opportunities with focus on education, health, skilling, and access to markets. Eg-PM-JANMAN for tribal inclusion.
2.    Balanced regional growth with targeted interventions.
3.    Income security - Social protection systems like MGNREGA, NFSA, PM-KISAN reduce vulnerability and support inclusive livelihoods.
4.    Strengthens financial inclusion - Eg- PM Jan Dhan Yojana opened 500 million+ bank accounts
5.    Equality of Opportunity - Eg- the Rights of Persons with Disabilities (RPwD) Act, 2016
6.    Ayushman Bharat: Provided free healthcare to 23 crore people.

### Inclusive Growth Ensuring Sustainability

1.    Affordable and clean energy (SDG 7) - PM Ujjwala Yojana distributed 10 crore LPG connections
2.    Encourages sustainable consumption and production patterns (SDG 12). Eg- Mission LiFE
3.    Supports protection of natural resources-forests, soil, and biodiversity (SDG 15). Eg: Compensatory Afforestation Funds
4.    Sustainable Livelihoods - Promotes climate-resilient agriculture, water conservation, and diversified livelihoods.
5.    Institutional Sustainability (SDG 16, SDG 17) through decentralisation, cooperative federalism and data-driven governance. Eg- Aspirational Districts Programme.

### Interlinking between Inclusiveness and Sustainability

1.    Inequality weakens long-term economic growth
2.    Environmental degradation hits the poorest hardest - Eg- Disaster induced Migration
3.    Inclusive growth strengthens environmental stewardship
4.    Sustainable livelihoods reduce vulnerability
5.    Intergenerational equity depends on both

### Challenges to Inclusive Growth under a Market Economy

1.    Rising inequality- Eg- the top 1% control 40% of net personal wealth.
2.    Regional disparities due to unequal investment and infrastructure. Eg- BIMARU States
3.    Jobless growth - Service sector contributes 55% of GDP but employs less than 30% workforce
4.    Weak social protection for informal workers (over 85% of India’s workforce).
5.    Market failures in public goods. Eg- Digital Apartheid in Education

### Way Forward

1.    Capability Approach (Amartya Sen) - increase Education and health spending to 6% and 2.5% of GDP respectively
2.    Strengthen progressive taxes, wealth taxes and targeted subsidies to reduce income inequality and expand welfare spending.
3.    Align national policies with Paris Agreement targets
4.    Universalise social security, pensions, maternity benefits, and unemployment allowance
** A nexus approach towards sustainability and inclusiveness is needed for ‘Sabka Saath, Sabka Vikas.’**

---

## Question 11 (Year: 2020 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2020-gs3-q11-civilsdaily**

** Explain intra-generational and inter-generational issues of equity from the perspective of inclusive growth and sustainable development. [Year: 2020] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

Inclusive growth and sustainable development emphasise ** fair distribution of opportunities, resources, and benefits **both ** within the present generation **and ** across future generations **.

### Intra-Generational Equity issues (Equity Within the Present Generation)

1.    Income and Wealth Inequality - the top 1% of adults in India control almost 40% of net personal wealth. (World Inequality Report)
2.    Social Exclusion - Caste, gender, disability, and minority identity restrict access to education, jobs, assets. Eg- Glass Ceiling for Women
3.    Poorer communities face greater vulnerability to pollution, floods, heatwaves, violating equity. Eg- Disaster induced migration
4.    Regional disparities - Eg- BIMARU States lag behind national averages in health, education and income.
5.    Low female labour force participation (41% vs 48% global average) limits inclusive access to economic opportunities.

### Inter-Generational Equity issues (Equity Across Future Generations)

1.    Over-extraction of Natural

** Resources -**Unsustainable mining, groundwater depletion, deforestation restrict future access.
Child grows up
Family in poverty
in poverty
2.    Climate change burden on future generations - Eg- Rising sea levels threatening the survival of low-lying island countries.
3.    Low social mobility- Eg - India ranks 76th in the Global Social Mobility Index (WEF), indicating persistence of inequality across generations.
4.    Failing to invest in research, innovation, and human capital reduces competitiveness of future generations. (R&D investment only 0.7% of GDP)
5.    Fiscal Burden - Unsustainable borrowing today limits fiscal space for future welfare and development spending.

### Way Forward

5.    Capability Approach (Amartya Sen) - increase Education and health spending to 6% and 2.5% of GDP respectively
6.    Strengthen progressive taxes, wealth taxes and targeted subsidies to reduce income inequality and expand welfare spending.
7.    Align national policies with Paris Agreement targets
8.    Universalise social security, pensions, maternity benefits, and unemployment allowance
** A nexus approach **towards sustainability and inclusiveness is needed for **‘Sabka Saath, Sabka Vikas.’**

## Question 12 (Year: 2022 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2022-gs3-q12-civilsdaily**

** Is inclusive growth possible under market economy ? State the significance of financial inclusion in achieving economic growth in India. [Year: 2022] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

As per ** OECD **, inclusive growth is economic growth distributed fairly across society and creates opportunities for all. A market economy drives ** efficiency and innovation **, but without corrective policies it can ** widen inequalities **.

### Inclusive Growth under Market Economy

1.    Efficient Resource Allocation- improve productivity, reduce costs, and expand economic opportunities.
2.    Market economies enable entrepreneurship, MSME growth and innovation-driven jobs. Eg- Indian start-up ecosystem.
3.    State as an Enabler- Government gets resources to invest in public goods.
4.    Property rights, contract enforcement and regulatory frameworks ensure fairness.
5.    Technological development enabling inclusive development - Eg- DBT.

### Challenges to Inclusive Growth under a Market Economy

6.    Rising inequality- Eg- the top 1% control 40% of net personal wealth.
7.    Regional disparities due to unequal investment and infrastructure. Eg- BIMARU States
8.    Jobless growth - Service sector contributes 55% of GDP but employs less than 30% workforce
9.    Weak social protection for informal workers (over 85% of India’s workforce).
10.    Market failures in public goods. Eg- Digital Apartheid in Education

### Significance of Financial Inclusion in Achieving Economic Growth in India

1.    Enhanced credit access for MSMEs, SHGs - boosts investment and employment. Eg. PM MUDRA has sanctioned over ₹22 lakh crore since inception.
2.    Greater savings through Jan Dhan accounts (53 crore accounts) ensures financial stability
3.    Formalisation of the economy via UPI, GSTN, Aadhaar - wider tax base and better compliance.
4.    Poverty reduction through targeted DBT, eliminating leakages and improving consumption.
5.    Women’s economic empowerment through SHG-bank linkage, Stand-Up India, digital microcredit - raises household productivity.
6.    Rural economic growth through Kisan Credit Cards, PM-Kisan and digital banking in villages.
7.    Improved risk management via insurance (PMJJBY, PMSBY) and pensions (PM-SYM) - stabilises vulnerable households.
8.    Boost to digital economy with UPI handling over f27 lakh crore monthly transactions - strengthens service sector growth.
Inclusive growth under a market economy is possible when markets are balanced with ** public investment, regulation and financial inclusion **.

---

## Question 13 (Year: 2022 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2022-gs3-q13-civilsdaily**

** The increase in life expectancy in the country has led to newer health challenges in the community. What are those challenges and what steps need to be taken to meet them? [Year: 2022] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

India’s life expectancy has increased to **72.4 years (2025)** due to better healthcare and nutrition. However, an ageing population brings ** new epidemiological, economic and social health challenges **.
** Healthy life expectancy in India is a decade behind overall life expectancy in 2022.**

### Newer Health Challenges Emerging from Higher Life Expectancy

1.    Rise in Non-Communicable Diseases (NCDs) - Higher prevalence of diabetes, hypertension, heart disease, cancer, dementia cause over 65% of total deaths in India.
2.    Geriatric Health Issues
•    Increase in frailty, vision/hearing loss, arthritis.

•    Growing burden of neurodegenerative disorders like Alzheimer’s and Parkinson’s.
3.    Multi-morbidity - Elderly increasingly suffer from multiple chronic conditions, requiring continuous care.
4.    Mental Health Challenges
•    Rising cases of depression, loneliness, anxiety.
•    Lack of community-based mental health services.
5.    Treatment of chronic and age-related conditions increases out-of-pocket expenditure.
6.    Weak Elderly Care Infrastructure - Limited geriatric wards, rehabilitation centres, home-care services and trained geriatric nurses.
7.    Feminization of aging - women face greater economic insecurity, inadequate pensions, elder abuse, and social isolation.

### Steps Needed to Address These Challenges

1.    Strengthen Geriatric Healthcare - Establish geriatric wards in district hospitals and train geriatric specialists
2.    Community-Based Care
•    Promote home healthcare and caregiver support services.
•    Incentivise care economy (eldercare workers, physiotherapists).
3.    National NCD Prevention Strategy
•    Screen population for hypertension, diabetes, cancers through HWCs.
•    Promote lifestyle interventions: yoga, diet counselling, tobacco control.
4.    Strengthen Mental Health Services
•    Expand Tele-MANAS, district mental health programmes.
•    Senior citizen counselling and social engagement platforms.
5.    Social Protection - Expand pension coverage by strengthening PM-SYM.
6.    Promote Active and Healthy Ageing through Fit India Movement.
7.    Data and Technology Integration - Use telemedicine, remote monitoring, and AI-based early diagnosis.
A forward-looking strategy that integrates healthcare, community support, and the silver economy will ensure that longer lives translate into ** healthier, dignified, and economically meaningful lives **.

---

## Question 14 (Year: 2024 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2024-gs3-q14-civilsdaily**

** Examine the (pattern) and trend of public expenditure on social services in the post-reforms period in India. To what extent this has been in consonance with achieving the objective of inclusive growth? [Year: 2024] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

Since the **1991 reforms **, India shifted to a market-oriented growth model. Public expenditure on social services increased from **5% of GDP (1990s)** to **8% (2024-25)**

### Trend of Public Expenditure on Social Services in the Post-Reforms Period

1.    Early Post-Reform Phase (1991-2005)
•    Low and stagnant spending around 5% of GDP due to fiscal consolidation.
•    Prioritisation of basic education - expansion of SSA, mid-day meal.
•    Health expenditure remained low at 1% of GDP, high OOPE.
2.    Rights-Based Expansion Phase (2005-2015)
•    Public expenditure rose to 6-7% of GDP.
•    Introduction of major rights-based entitlements: MGNREGA (2005), RTI, RTE (2009), NFSA (2013).
•    Focus on rural livelihood missions, inclusion programmes. Eg- DAY-NRLM
3.    Post-2015 Period
•    Social sector spending increased to 8% of GDP (2021-22).
•    Health spending reforms - decline in OOPE from 65% to 40% (2014-2024).
•    Women Specific schemes: Eg- Ujjwala (10 crore LPG connections)
•    Emphasis on social security. Eg- e-Shram, PM Garib Kalyan Anna Yojana.
•    Increased focus on skill development, digital inclusion. Eg- JAM Trinity, PM-KVY

### In consonance with Inclusive Growth

1.    Extreme poverty fell from 16.2 % in 2011-12 to just 2.3 % in 2022-23
2.    MGNREGA, NFSA ensured income security and food security (67% population coverage).
3.    Human Capital Improvement - Life expectancy increased from 58 years (1990) to 73 years.
4.    Regional Inclusion - Aspirational Districts improved health, education, and infrastructure indicators in 112 lagging districts.
5.    Women Empowerment - Eg- 45% women representation in PRIs

### Limitations and Challenges

1.    Rural-Urban Divide Persists - Urban per capita income is 2x rural.
2.    Only 24-25% of the population has any formal social protection.
3.    Poor Learning Outcomes
•    50% of Class 5 students cannot read Class 2 text (ASER).
•    50% of graduates are employable only (India Skills Report).
4.    Low Public Health Spending - Still around 1.9% of GDP, below the global average of 6%.
5.    Inclusion-Exclusion errors and Leakages in PDS.
6.    High Inequality - Top 10% hold 77% of national wealth (Oxfam).
** Capability Approach (Amartya Sen) by **increasing Education and health spending to 6% and 2.5% of GDP respectively is needed for **‘Sabka Saath, Sabka Vikas.’**

---

## Question 15 (Year: 2025 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2025-gs3-q15-civilsdaily**

** Distinguish between the Human Development Index (HDI) and the Inequality-adjusted Human Development Index (IHDI) with special reference to India. Why is the IHDI considered a better indicator of inclusive growth? [Year: 2025] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

The ** Human Development Index (HDI) **, introduced by UNDP in 1990, measures a country’s progress in terms of ** health, education, and income **. The ** Inequality-adjusted Human Development Index (IHDI) **, introduced in 2010, refines HDI by factoring in ** inequality of distribution **of these achievements.

|** Aspect **|** HDI (Human Development Index) **|** IHDI (Inequality-adjusted HDI) **|
|** Definition **| Composite index of ** life expectancy, education, and per capita income **. | Adjusts HDI downward by factoring in ** inequality in distribution **of these dimensions. |
|** Focus **| Measures ** average achievements **of a nation. | Measures ** actual distribution **of achievements among people. |
|** Nature of**
** Measurement **| Reflects ** potential human development **if everyone had equal access. | Reflects ** real human development **after inequality is accounted for. |
|** Treatment of Inequality **| Ignores disparities across class, gender, or region. | Captures inequality and reduces score proportionately. |
|** Strengths **| Simple, easy for global comparison, long time series available. | More realistic, shows inclusiveness, better for policy planning. |
|** Limitations **| Masks exclusion, can overestimate progress in unequal societies. | More data-intensive, not available historically for all countries. |
|** India’s Ranking **| HDI score =**0.685 **, Rank **130/193 **. | IHDI score =**0.537 **. |

### India’s Human Development Performance

1.    Human Development Index (HDI)

•    Rank improved from 133 (2022) ^ 130 (2023) out of 193 countries.
•    Since 1990, HDI improved by 53%, outpacing global and South Asian averages.
2.    Inequality-adjusted Human Development Index (IHDI)
•    India suffers a 30.7% loss due to inequality.
•    Poorest 40% hold only 20.2% of income, while the richest 10% hold 25.5%.

### Why IHDI is a Better Indicator of Inclusive Growth

1.    Accounts for Inequality - Unlike HDI, IHDI reduces scores based on income, education, and health disparities, showing the real distribution of gains.
2.    Closer to Ground Reality - Reflects what people actually experience, not just national averages. For India, 30.7% loss of human development due to inequality.
3.    Reveals Hidden Gaps - Exposes divides across region, caste, class, and gender that HDI alone masks. Eg- gender gap in Labour Force Participation Rate
4.    Guides Policy Better - Eg- targeted schemes like PM Poshan Abhiyan or Eklavya Model Schools
5.    Captures Inter-generational Equity - By highlighting disparities, it stresses need for equal opportunities for long-term human development.
6.    Comparative Value - Countries with similar HDI can differ widely in IHDI, revealing which societies are more inclusive.
7.    Supports SDGs - Aligns with SDG 10 (Reduce Inequality) and SDG 1 (No Poverty) by showing inequality-adjusted outcomes.

As Amartya Sen observed, “Development is about expanding freedoms.” HDI shows progress, but IHDI shows whether that progress is fairly shared.

---

## Question 16 (Year: 2019 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2019-gs3-q16-civilsdaily**

** Enumerate the indirect taxes which have been subsumed in the goods and services tax (GST) in India. Also, comment on the revenue implications of the GST introduced in India since July 2017. [Year: 2019] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

The Goods and Services Tax (GST), implemented on **1 July 2017 **, unified India’s fragmented indirect tax system into a ** single, destination-based tax **, aimed at creating a **‘one nation, one tax’** System.

### Indirect Taxes Subsumed under GST

|** Central Taxes Subsumed **|** State Taxes Subsumed **|
|**•    Central Excise Duty - Except on petroleum and tobacco.**

**•    Service Tax**

**•    Additional Excise Duties**

**•    Countervailing Duty (CVD)**

**•    Special Additional Duty (SAD)**

**•    Central Surcharges & Cesses related to supply of goods and services.**|**•    State VAT/Sales Tax - Except on petroleum products.**

**•    Entertainment Tax**

**•    Luxury Tax**

**•    Entry Tax/Octroi**

**•    Purchase Tax**

**•    Taxes on Lottery, Betting, Gambling **|

** Revenue Implications of GST Since July 2017**
1.    Expansion of Tax Base- Eg - over 1.51 crore active GST registrations. (65 lakh in 2017)
2.    Rising Revenue Collections - Eg - Average monthly collections rose from ?90,000 crore (2017-18) to ?1.92 lakh crore+ (2025).

8 YEARS OF GST
A look at Gross GST Collections (in ? lakh crore)
3.    Formalisation - E-invoicing, ITC matching and GSTN integration improved compliance, pushing MSMEs into the formal economy

Source: Ministry of Finance
4.    Reduction in Cascading - Unified tax with seamless input credit reduced the tax-on-tax effect, improving supply-chain efficiency and indirectly boosting revenues.
5.    Support for Manufacturing: Correcting inverted duty structures enhances domestic value addition, strengthens export competitiveness, and boosts revenue.
6.    Ease of Compliance - lower rates under GST 2.0 combined with better compliance can increase GST collections in the medium term.

### Challenges

1.    Post GST 2.0 revenue shortfall of ?48,000 crore annually is predicted. Due to reduced rates and zero-rating of many goods.

Fiscal Year
2.    PRS Report- the aggregate revenue under GST has declined
** from 6.5% of GDP in 2015-16 **to **5.5% of GDP in 2023-24 **. (** below the 7% GST-to-GDP ratio **projected by the **15th FC)**
3.    Initial Revenue Volatility - States faced shortfalls despite compensation, indicating
4.    High Compliance Burden - Multiple monthly, quarterly, and annual returns, e-invoicing, and ITC reconciliation increase administrative load, especially for SMEs.
5.    State Revenue Concerns - Dependence on compensation cess and delays in payments strain state finances
6.    Evasion and fraud through fraudulent activities like fake invoices persist.
7.    Nearly half of the economy remains outside the GST framework. Eg- petroleum products, real estate, and electricity duties are excluded from GST.
For ** higher, predictable and efficient **revenue generation, the need is to
1.    Include petroleum and electricity under the GST
2.    Anti-Evasion Measures: Eg- Utilizing advanced data analytics
3.    Bring emerging sectors- crypto-assets, carbon credits under GST

## Question 17 (Year: 2019 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2019-gs3-q17-civilsdaily**

** The public expenditure management is a challenge to the Government of India in the context of budget making during the post liberalization period. Clarify it. [Year: 2019] [Marks: 15]**

### Answer

** Post-1991 liberalisation **transformed India’s economy from a ** state-controlled **to a more ** market-driven **system. This expanded public ** spending needs **while simultaneously demanding ** fiscal discipline.**

### Need for Public Expenditure

1.    Provision of Public Goods - Eg- spending on health, education

2.    Social Welfare & Equity - Eg - Poshan 2.0, PM-Jan Arogya Yojana.
3.    Infrastructure Development - Eg - National Infrastructure Pipeline.
4.    Poverty Alleviation & Employment - Eg - MGNREGA wage payments.
5.    Reducing Regional Imbalances - Eg - Aspirational Districts Programme.
6.    Counter-cyclical spending during downturns. - Eg - Pandemic stimulus packages.
7.    Human Capital Development - Eg - PM Kaushal Vikas Yojana.
8.    Technological & R&D Support - Eg - Funding for ISRO, Digital India.

### Major challenges in Public Expenditure management

1.    High Subsidies and Social Welfare Programs: The subsidy bill for FY 2025-26 was estimated at ₹4.26 lakh crores. (3% of GDP)

2.    Interest Payment obligations - The budgetary estimate for 2025-26 - Rs 12.76 lakh crore on interest payments forming 25 % of the government's total expenditure.
3.    Low Tax Buoyancy: The tax-to-GDP ratio in India is around 10-12%, lower, while for OECD its 33%.
4.    Expanding Welfare Commitments - Growth in health, education, pensions, MGNREGA raises recurring liabilities.
5.    FRBM Constraints - FRBM mandates FD of 4.4% of GDP, limiting fiscal space.
6.    Poor Budgetary Forecasting : Budgets often overstate revenue projections (15 out of 20 years since fiscal 1998) and understate expenditures (12 out of 20 years since fiscal 1998).
7.    Fiscal Populism - eg loan waivers to farmers
8.    Rise in Off-Budget Expenditure - Eg: Food subsidy via FCI, UDAY bonds by states.
9.    Rise in Public Administration Costs - Eg: 8th Pay Commission can increase salary & pension burden.
10.    Need for Infrastructure Investment in transport, energy, and urbanisation, but fiscal space remained limited. Eg- As per WB, $2.2 trillion by 2030 is needed
11.    Public Sector Inefficiencies - Persistent losses in PSUs require budgetary support, reducing room for developmental expenditure.

### External Challenges

1.    Volatile Crude Oil Prices due to geopolitical instability. India imports 85% of its crude.
2.    Rising International Commitments under Paris Agreement, SDGs, Sendai Framework etc. Eg-Renewable energy targets.
3.    Rupee depreciation increases the cost of external debt servicing and capital imports.
4.    Rising Protectionism and Trade Wars have impacted exports. Eg- Trump H1B visa restrictions
5.    Increased Defence spending due to External Threat. Eg- 5% increase in defence spending in 2025 than 2024.

### Way Forward for Effective Fiscal Policy in India

1.    Establish an independent fiscal council to provide unbiased analysis of fiscal policy and enhance transparency and accountability. (15th FC Report)
2.    Scrutiny of Populist Policies and Outcome-Oriented Budgeting (NITI Aayog)
3.    Leveraging PPP for mobilizing private sector investment for infrastructure projects. (Economic Survey)
4.    Reforming Social Welfare Programs: Eg- Shanta Kumar Committee estimated that reforms in PDS could save f 10,000 crores.                                           ___
5.    Cut down administrative costs by 10-15% through e-governance. (2nd ARC)^
6.    Enhance Tax Buoyancy - to achieve a medium-term growth trajectory of 6.5-7.0% and realize Viksit Bharat vision, tax buoyancy needs to be in the 1.2-1.5 range. (EY Report)
7.    Improve Centre-State Fiscal Coordination - Encourage states through capex-linked incentives, as in Union Budget 2023-24’s 50-year interest-free loans.
8.    Strategic Disinvestment - Use proceeds to fund infrastructure, logistics, transport, not for recurring expenditure. (NITI Aayog)
Efficient expenditure is critical for sustainable budgeting and ** Viksit Bharat 2047.**

---

## Question 18 (Year: 2020 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2020-gs3-q18-civilsdaily**

** Explain the rationale behind the Goods and Services Tax (Compensation to States) Act of 2017. How has COVID-19 impacted the GST compensation fund and created new federal tensions? [Year: 2020] [Marks: 15]**

### Answer

The GST, implemented on **1 July 2017 **, unified India’s fragmented indirect tax system into a ** single, destination-based tax **, aimed at creating a **‘one nation, one tax’** System.

### Rationale behind the GST (Compensation to States) Act, 2017

1.    GST subsumed major state taxes (VAT, entry tax, octroi). To prevent short-term revenue loss, the Act assured 14% annual revenue growth for 5 years (2017-22).
2.    Addressing Loss of Fiscal Autonomy - Compensation ensured states’ fiscal stability during structural shifts.
3.    Cooperative Federalism- States agreed to adopt GST in exchange for legal assurance of compensation from the Centre.
4.    Creating Predictability in Budgeting - Guaranteed revenue helped states plan welfare schemes, salaries, and capital projects without fear of instability.
5.    Compensation Fund Mechanism- A dedicated GST Compensation Cess (on luxury/sin goods like tobacco, coal, automobiles) was created to finance the compensation pool.

### Impact of COVID-19 on the GST Compensation Fund

1.    According to the 41st GST Council meeting, states projected a GST compensation requirement of f3 lakh crore for 2020-21. With an estimated compensation cess collection of only f65,000 crore, the shortfall in the GST compensation fund was expected to be f2.35 lakh crore.
•    f97,000 crore was due to GST implementation-related revenue gaps, and
•    f 1.38 lakh crore was attributed to the COVID-19-induced economic shock
2.    The Centre admitted an unprecedented shortfall, stating it could not fully compensate states from the fund.
3.    Borrowing Controversy
•    The Centre asked states to borrow via RBI under two options.
•    Many states (Kerala, Punjab, Chhattisgarh) argued that the borrowing burden should lie with the Centre, not states.
4.    Breakdown of Consensus in GST Council - For the first time since 2017, the Council saw voting instead of consensus. States alleged weakening of cooperative federalism.
5.    Increased Fiscal Stress on States - Shortfalls forced states to cut capital expenditure, delay welfare payments, and increase market borrowing.
6.    States demanded extending the compensation period beyond June 2022 due to pandemic losses Strengthening the fiscal framework, improving tax buoyancy, and enhancing transparency in compensation mechanisms are essential to restore trust in India’s cooperative federalism.

---

## Question 19 (Year: 2021 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2021-gs3-q19-civilsdaily**

** Distinguish between Capital Budget and Revenue Budget. Explain the components of both these Budgets. [Year: 2021] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

Under Article 112, the Budget comprises the ** Revenue Budget **, which covers routine government income and expenditure, and the ** Capital Budget **, which deals with asset creation and long-term liabilities.
** Difference Between Revenue Budget and Capital Budget**

|** Basis **|** Revenue Budget **|** Capital Budget **|
|** Nature **| Relates to ** regular income and expenditure **| Relates to ** asset creation and long-term investments **|
|** Objective **| Meet recurring expenditure and collect revenues | Building long term capital base |
|** Components **| Includes ** tax **and non-** tax **revenue |** Borrowings **, ** loan recoveries, **and ** disinvestment receipts **|
|** Assets/Liabilities **|** Does not **create assets or liabilities |** Creates assets **or ** raises liabilities **|
|** Duration Effect **| Short-term, recurring | Long-term, developmental |
|** Examples **| Salaries, pensions, subsidies | Infrastructure investment, loans, repayment |

** Components of the Revenue Budget**
A.    Revenue Receipts
1.    Tax Revenue - Income tax, corporate tax, GST, customs, excise, etc.
2.    Non-Tax Revenue - Dividends & profits from PSUs/RBI, fees, fines, interest receipts.
3.    Grants-in-Aid - External grants from other countries/institutions.
B.    Revenue Expenditure
1.    Salaries, Pensions & Administrative Costs
2.    Subsidies - food, fertiliser, petroleum.
3.    Interest Payments on past borrowings.
4.    Grants to States & UTs, grants for social services.
5.    Expenditure on Routine Government Operations - police, defence services (revenue), judiciary. Components of the Capital Budget
A.    Capital Receipts
1.    Borrowings - Market loans, external loans, treasury bills.
2.    Disinvestment Proceeds - Sale of government equity in PSUs.
3.    Recovery of Loans - Repayment from states, PSUs, and others.
4.    Small Savings & Provident Fund Collections
B.    Capital Expenditure
1.    Creation of Assets - Roads, railways, bridges, irrigation, defence capital.
2.    Loans and Advances - To states, UTs, PSUs, and financial institutions.
3.    Investment in PSUs and Infrastructure Projects
A healthy fiscal structure requires ** containing revenue expenditure **and prioritising ** capital expenditure **to strengthen productivity and economic growth.

---

## Question 20 (Year: 2025 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2025-gs3-q20-civilsdaily**

** Explain how the Fiscal Health Index (FHI) can be used as a tool for assessing the fiscal performance of states in India. In what way would it encourage the states to adopt prudent and sustainable fiscal policies? [Year: 2025] [Marks: 15]**

### Answer

The ** Fiscal Health Index (FHI) **initiative by ** NITI Aayog **evaluates the fiscal health of ** eighteen major states **through a composite index using data from the ** CAG **, covering the ** Financial Year 2022-23 **.

### FHI as a tool to assess fiscal performance of states

1.    FHI uses uniform metrics-Tax Buoyancy, Debt-to-GSDP, Fiscal Deficit, Capex Share-allowing objective comparison across states.
2.    Multi-dimensional Evaluation - Covers five pillars and reveal structural strengths and weakness
3.    Measures states’ ability to mobilise resources through Own Tax Revenue (OTR) and Own Non-tax Revenue (ONTR). Eg - Higher OTR-to-GSDP ratio reflects stronger fiscal autonomy.
4.    Measures Quality of Expenditure - FHI differentiates between capital expenditure and revenue expenditure. Eg - States like Gujarat and Karnataka show higher capex ratios.
5.    Tracks Debt Sustainability - Assesses Debt-GSDP ratio, interest payment burden, and future liabilities. Eg - FHI flags high-debt states such as Punjab, Kerala, Rajasthan, and West Bengal.
6.    Monitors Fiscal Deficit and Compliance with FRBM Limits - Shows whether states adhere to 3% fiscal deficit glide path.
7.    Identifies Risk from Off-Budget Borrowings - Captures liabilities from power sector guarantees, state PSUs, and special purpose vehicles.
8.    Highlights Best Practices - Eg- Top states-Odisha (67.8 score), Chhattisgarh, Goa-show strong non-tax revenue, low fiscal deficits, and high capital outlays

### Role of FHI in Encouraging prudent and sustainable fiscal policies

1.    Promotes Fiscal Discipline - Poor rankings push states to reduce deficits and unsustainable borrowing.
2.    Incentivises Capital Spending - Encourages a shift from populist revenue expenditure towards productive capital outlay.
3.    Supports Long-Term Planning - Aligns state finances with sustainable development goals and resilience-building.
4.    Revenue Reforms-Stimulates states to improve tax buoyancy, and non-tax revenue mobilisation
5.    Drives Structural Reforms like subsidy rationalization, reduction in revenue leakages etc.
6.    Transparency & Accountability - Public scrutiny builds pressure on governments for fiscal prudence
7.    Encourages Inter-State Competition - Rankings foster a competitive spirit to achieve stronger fiscal performance.
8.    Strengthens Cooperative Federalism - Helps in Centre-State dialogue on shared fiscal risks and sustainability.
9.    Boosts Investor Confidence - Strong fiscal performance signals creditworthiness, attracting investment.
10.    Promotes Sustainable Borrowing Practices and enhances creditworthiness as better FHI improves a state's credit rating.

### Challenges

1.    Data Gaps - CAG data of Financial Year 2022-23 used
2.    Off-budget borrowings not fully captured in FHI.
3.    Miss qualitative aspects such as governance quality, efficiency of welfare delivery etc.
4.    Inter-State Structural Variations are not fully captured - Eg- Resource-rich states (Odisha, Chhattisgarh) naturally perform better in non-tax revenues
5.    Competitive Populism reduces focus on fiscal discipline. Eg- farm loan waivers
6.    Weak Enforcement - FHI rankings have no binding effect on policy behaviour.
By encouraging ** disciplined, sustainable, and quality spending **, FHI can help realise the vision of ** Viksit Bharat@2047**

---

## Question 21 (Year: 2023 | Paper: GS III)
** Question ID: 2023-gs3-q21-civilsdaily**

** Faster economic growth requires increased share of the manufacturing sector in GDP, particularly of MSMEs. Comment on the present policies of the Government in this regard. [Year: 2023] [Marks: 10]**

### Answer

MSME sector contributes “nearly one-third to India’s GDP” and is a “key pillar” of growth. However, the share of manufacturing in GDP has remained stagnant at **17%** since the last 3 decades.

### Importance of increasing the manufacturing share (especially MSMEs)

1.    Strong employment multiplier: MMSME employes over 20Cr people
2.    Better capital-output ratio than large heavy industries.
3.    Inclusive industrialisation: Eg- 20% are MSMEs are owned by women
4.    Global value-chain linkages: MSMEs account for 45.7% of India’s total exports
5.    Structural transformation: A higher manufacturing share signals shift towards higher-productivity sectors.

### Present policies of government to boost MSME manufacturing

1.    Revised MSME definition & registration via Udyam Registration
2.    Improve infrastructure and logistics - Gati Shakti Program, National Logistics Policy

3.    Production Linked Incentive (PLI) Scheme to attract investment and boost domestic manufacturing.
4.    Ease of doing business through labour reforms. Eg- 4 labour courts
5.    Over 2.5 lakh MSMEs onboarded on GeM for direct government procurement.
6.    MSME SAMADHAAN portal for grievance redressal related to delayed payments.
7.    Udyam Registration system to access government benefits and schemes.

### Issues

1.    Missing middle problem - 95% Micro Businesses
2.    Slow to transition to advanced technology. (Ficci-Mckinsey Report)
3.    Infrastructure Deficiencies -
•    Multimodal connectivity gaps.
•    Power outages, weak water supply, and poor transport networks.

•    Land acquisition delays

•    High compliance cost ?13-17 lakh/year per MSME
5.    Limited Access to Finance (only 14% out of 64 million)
Recommendations of ** UK Sinha Committee** must be implemented to make India the global manufacturing hub
